    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="ffe3ebcc1b7146f8bfe48d25d55164b3">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '0357bf06-506d-46e1-b705-fdd5b9671a61'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Zhu/Jianhui">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Mi/Hongwei" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Hongwei Mi<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d176113499" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Transition metal chalcogenide (TMD) electrodes in sodium-ion batteries exhibit intrinsic shortcomings such as sluggish reaction kinetics, unstable conversion thermodynamics, and substantial volumetric strain effects, which lead to electrochemical failure. This report unlocks a design paradigm of VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C in-plane heterojunction with built-in anion vacancy, achieved through an in situ functionalization and self-limited growth approach. Theoretical and experimental investigations reveal the bifunctional role of the Se vacancy in enhancing the ion diffusion kinetics and the structural thermodynamics of Na<sub>x</sub>VSe<sub>2</sub> active phases. Moreover, this in-plane heterostructure facilitates complete face contact between the two components and tight interfacial conductive contact between the conversion phases, resulting in enhanced reaction reversibility. The VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C heterojunction electrode exhibits remarkable sodium-ion storage performance, retaining specific capacities of 448.7 and 424.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at current densities of 5 and 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Moreover, it exhibits a high specific capacity of 353.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even under the demanding condition of 100 A g<sup>−1</sup>, surpassing most previous achievements. The proposed strategy can be extended to other V<sub>5</sub>S<sub>8−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> and V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-based heterojunctions, marking a conceptual breakthrough in advanced electrode design for constructing high-performance sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202310336-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202310336-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Transitioning to renewable energy, as opposed to fossil fuels, is imperative for sustainable development. Sodium-ion batteries, with their abundant resources and lower costs compared to lithium-ion batteries, as well as higher energy density than traditional lead-acid batteries, present promising solutions for future energy storage needs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0001_R_d176113491e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Sustainability">1</a>-<a href="#adma202310336-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0004_R_d176113491e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nano-Micro Lett.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The critical challenge lies in identifying high-capacity and highly reversible electrode materials, particularly for anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0005_R_d176113491e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a>-<a href="#adma202310336-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0007_R_d176113491e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Various anode candidates, such as hard carbon,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0008_R_d176113491e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#adma202310336-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0009_R_d176113491e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> metal oxides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0010_R_d176113491e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">10</a>, <a href="#adma202310336-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0011_R_d176113491e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> alloys,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0012_R_d176113491e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and layered transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs), have been explored.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0013_R_d176113491e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a>-<a href="#adma202310336-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0017_R_d176113491e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Nano">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among these, TMDs stand out due to their favorable ionic conductivity, editable interface structure, and weak van der Waals force-connected adjacent layer structure, allowing for efficient Na<sup>+</sup> storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0018_R_d176113491e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Environ. Sci.">18</a>-<a href="#adma202310336-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0022_R_d176113491e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, their practical application is limited by their capacity and cycle performance. The electrochemical failure mechanism indicates that this suboptimal performance is closely tied to significant volumetric strain effects and poor reaction kinetics and thermodynamics during cycling, resulting in irreversible intercalation and conversion reactions, ultimately leading to battery failure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0023_R_d176113491e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a>-<a href="#adma202310336-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0025_R_d176113491e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, achieving high-performance TMD-based anode materials necessitates improving reaction reversibility through rational structural design, enhancing reaction kinetics, and improving thermodynamics.</p>
         
         <p>Researchers have proposed numerous feasible strategies, including composite construction,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0026_R_d176113491e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Nano">26</a>, <a href="#adma202310336-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0027_R_d176113491e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> vacancy engineering,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0028_R_d176113491e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Eng. J.">28</a>, <a href="#adma202310336-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0029_R_d176113491e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Storage Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and morphological design.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0030_R_d176113491e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater.">30</a>-<a href="#adma202310336-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0033_R_d176113491e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, binary heterostructures, which exhibit the interface complementary effect, are effective in enhancing the Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability of TMD-based anode materials. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0001">1a</a>, layer-by-layer self-assembly, a physical mixing method, yields heterogeneous anode materials through liquid phase dispersion and extraction filtration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0034_R_d176113491e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Small">34</a>-<a href="#adma202310336-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0036_R_d176113491e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, Yang et al. fabricated V<sub>5</sub>S<sub>8</sub>-graphite hybrid nanosheets as an anode material with high rate capability for sodium-ion batteries, delivering a specific capacity of 344 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a high current density of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0037_R_d176113491e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Energy Environ. Sci.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the efficacy of this strategy is limited by low binding strength and self-agglomeration between each component. Another widely explored approach is the ex situ out-plane heterojunction growth.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0038_R_d176113491e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Chem. Eng. J.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Jiang et al. synthesized the MoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene heterostructure using MXene nanosheets as a growth substrate through a hydrothermal method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0039_R_d176113491e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Chem. Eng. J.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These resulting electrodes, applied to sodium-ion batteries, exhibited high reversible capacities of 490 and 250 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 1 and 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, surpassing those of pure MoSe<sub>2</sub> electrodes. While the line-to-face complete contact of components strengthens the electrode's reaction kinetics, the vertical growth of the active component reduces the interaction between binary members, resulting in only moderate enhancement.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0040_R_d176113491e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Energy Storage Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The future direction entails simultaneously achieving high binding strength and a high degree of interaction among heterogeneous components for a substantial enhancement effect. Moreover, superior TMDs-based anode materials capable of withstanding ultrafast-charging conditions, especially under harsh conditions exceeding 50 A g<sup>−1</sup>, must be explored for developing high-performance sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202310336-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6b10fb70-17a8-42df-929d-c78708a57ba2/adma202310336-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6b10fb70-17a8-42df-929d-c78708a57ba2/adma202310336-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5f77c538-d631-412d-83d3-4fa065b53c98/adma202310336-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6b10fb70-17a8-42df-929d-c78708a57ba2/adma202310336-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310336-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310336" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Comparison of different building patterns of binary heterogeneous anode materials, including layer-by-layer self-assembly, ex situ out-plane heterojunction, and in situ in-plane heterojunction growth. b) Schematic diagram of the structural evolution of the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C in-plane heterojunction during in situ selenization and self-limited growth on a multifunctional V<sub>2</sub>CT<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene template.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>This report presents a unique in situ functionalization and self-limited growth method for designing VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C in-plane heterojunction with built-in anion vacancies, using V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene as a multifunctional template. During selenization (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0001">1b</a>), the carbon atoms layer of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> transforms into ultrathin conductive carbon, maximizing face-to-face contact with the generated VSe<sub>2</sub> component. Simultaneously, the spontaneous formation of a carbon reduction environment weakens the V─Se bond, creating an abundant Se anion vacancy structure, as verified through multiple characterizations Moreover, a multiscale interfacial synergy is disclosed between the bifunctional anion vacancy and the in-plane heterostructure, mitigating the volumetric strain and enhancing the kinetics and thermodynamics of Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and subsequent conversion reactions. Therefore, the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C in-plane heterojunction emerges as a superior sodium-ion battery anode material with superfast storage capability, maintaining high specific capacities of 424.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Remarkably, it exhibits impressive capacities of 392.5, 363.9, and 353.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 50, 80, and a record-high 100 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Furthermore, sodium-ion full batteries with VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C anodes and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathodes exhibit good cycle performance in both 25 and 0&nbsp;°C environments.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202310336-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202310336-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0002">2a</a> depicts the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of pure VSe<sub>2</sub> and the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C in-plane heterojunction. Both samples exhibit similar diffraction peaks, closely matching the PDF standard card (JCPDS No. 89-1641). This alignment indicates the successful synthesis of a high-purity VSe<sub>2</sub> phase after selenization using the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene template (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). However, notable shifts in the diffraction peaks of the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C sample towards smaller angles, such as the (001) and (011) planes, are observed. For example, the lattice spacing of the (011) plane increases from 0.262&nbsp;nm (VSe<sub>2</sub>) to 0.27&nbsp;nm (VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C). According to the result of ICP analysis (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), this phenomenon can be attributed to the creation of abundant Se vacancies inducing the formation of V<sup>3+</sup> with a larger radius than that of V<sup>4+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0041_R_d176113491e693" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequent electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) analysis, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0002">2c</a>, reveals a sharp fitting EPR signal at <i>g</i> = 2.003 in the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C, corresponding to the abundant Se vacancy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0016_R_d176113491e708" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">16</a>, <a href="#adma202310336-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0041_R_d176113491e708" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such defect structure was also studied by the positron annihilation lifetime spectroscopy (PALS) technique (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Then, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0002">2d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) compare the fitting results of V 2p and Se 3d XPS spectra of these two samples, respectively. For Se 3d XPS, the heterojunction sample shifts toward higher binding energy than that of the pure one, owing to the Se vacancy structure. Besides, unlike the existence of only V<sup>4+</sup> for VSe<sub>2</sub>, it was indicated that VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C shows the chemical states of V<sup>4+</sup> at 516.5 and 523.9&nbsp;eV, as well as the V<sup>3+</sup> at 513 and 520.5&nbsp;eV, respectively. Note that the reduction of average chemical valence of V is mainly to achieve charge balance after Se vacancy formation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0028_R_d176113491e739" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Eng. J.">28</a>, <a href="#adma202310336-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0042_R_d176113491e739" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nano Energy">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further insights from Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0002">2e</a>, showcasing Raman spectra of VSe<sub>2</sub> and VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C. On one hand, it can be seen that VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C has two distinct peaks at 1350 and 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup>, representing the D-band and G-band of the carbon component.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0041_R_d176113491e761" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, although these two samples exhibit similar peaks assigned to in-plane E<sub>g</sub> mode of 1T phase, A<sub>1g</sub> out-of-plane vibration mode and E<sub>2g</sub> in-plane vibration mode, the Se vacancy-induced bond softening between Se atoms would lead to the lower peak intensity and lower vibration frequency of VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0042_R_d176113491e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nano Energy">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The FESEM image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0002">2f</a> displays the nanosheet-like morphology of the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C in-plane heterojunction, maintaining similarity to V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, pure VSe<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information) exhibits a thick block structure. EDS elemental mapping (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information) confirms the uniform distribution of V, Se, and C signals in VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C. Additional structural characterization through TEM (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information), HRTEM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0002">2g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), and corresponding selected area electron diffraction (SAED) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0002">2h</a>) in conjunction with atomic force microscopy (AFM) measurement (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information) indicates the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C in-plane heterojunction with ultrathin lamellar structure (≈10&nbsp;nm) and 2D-2D heterostructure consisted of crystalline VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> and amorphous carbon regions. Consequently, the in situ self-limited growth of the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C in-plane heterojunction through direct selenization of the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene template is demonstrated. Beyond VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C, we also validate the extension of this design strategy to the construction of V<sub>5</sub>S<sub>8−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12–S14</a>, Supporting Information) and V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C in-plane heterojunctions (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information), showcasing significant potential in energy storage applications.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202310336-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/186588d5-0452-41ea-987c-1a7cf60e87b7/adma202310336-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/186588d5-0452-41ea-987c-1a7cf60e87b7/adma202310336-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4992dcd1-b5c9-49b3-abb6-c6a65ab87b51/adma202310336-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/186588d5-0452-41ea-987c-1a7cf60e87b7/adma202310336-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310336-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310336" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural and morphological characterizations of VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C in-plane heterojunction. a) XRD patterns of the pure VSe<sub>2</sub> and VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C, and b) corresponding locally enlarged patterns. c) EPR spectra, d) V 2p XPS spectra, and e) Raman spectra of the pure VSe<sub>2</sub> and VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C heterojunction. f) FESEM image, g) HRTEM image, and h) corresponding SAED image of the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p><b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0003">3a</a> depicts the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode in the 0.01–3.0&nbsp;V range at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, revealing several sharp cathodic peaks at 1.8, 1.66, and 0.4&nbsp;V during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion process in the initial cycle, which should correspond to the formation of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VSe<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>Se, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0017_R_d176113491e938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Nano">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the following Na<sup>+</sup> extraction process, three distinct anodic peaks appear at 1.51, 1.76, and 2.06&nbsp;V, corresponding to the recovery of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VSe<sub>2</sub> and VSe<sub>2</sub> phases.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0042_R_d176113491e953" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nano Energy">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Highly coincident redox peaks in subsequent cycles indicate the reversible multiple reactions after the initial activation, consistent with CV curves for the VSe<sub>2</sub> electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0003">3b</a> demonstrates the cycle performance test at a current density of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode delivers a specific capacity of 424.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles with a capacity retention of 104.04%, surpassing the pure VSe<sub>2</sub> counterpart with 170.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles. Interestingly, a gradual activation in the 50–250 cycles can be detected for VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C, such fading-reactivation phenomenon is probably driven by the delay infiltration of electrolyte into active component. Since the amorphous carbon region would create spatial obstruction for Na<sup>+</sup> insertion, when the high-crystalline component transforms into the discrete low-crystalline component upon cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0043_R_d176113491e986" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Mater. Chem. A">43</a>-<a href="#adma202310336-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0045_R_d176113491e986" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Energy Storage Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Corresponding galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) curves of VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0003">3c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information) exhibit three voltage platforms at around 1.8, 1.66, and 0.4&nbsp;V during the initial discharge process, aligning with the CV result. Almost coincidental GCD curves in subsequent cycles indicate high storage reversibility. A similar outcome is obtained at a current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information), maintaining a capacity of 448.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0003">3d</a> compares the storage performance of the two electrodes at various current densities. Notably, VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C demonstrates a high specific capacity of 353.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at the record-high current density of 100&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, retaining 98.92% of the capacity achieved at 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (493.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). Similar electrochemical behaviors in the corresponding GCD curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0003">3e</a>) during testing indicate the remarkable rate capability and superfast charging characteristics of the in-plane VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C heterojunction electrode. It should be pointed out that the proportion of selenium vacancy within VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C can be modulated by controlling the holding time during fabrication (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). However, the effect of only vacancy proportion on the performance of electrode is still difficult to uncover, other synergistic factors may need to be simultaneously considered in such composite system.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202310336-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/66e0bbc9-a4e4-44f7-af5e-f32de253a00a/adma202310336-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/66e0bbc9-a4e4-44f7-af5e-f32de253a00a/adma202310336-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/43bc2d4c-b084-45e9-9bde-9e76f98cd493/adma202310336-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/66e0bbc9-a4e4-44f7-af5e-f32de253a00a/adma202310336-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310336-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310336" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance test of the VSe<sub>2</sub> and VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C in-plane heterojunction electrodes. a) Cyclic voltammetry curvesof VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Cycle performance test at the current density of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and c) corresponding GCD curves of VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode in the initial cycles. d) Rate performance test and e) corresponding GCD curves of VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode at various current densities. f) Comparison of storage performance between this work and previously reported electrodes. g) Rate performance of the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C//NVP full cell. h) Cycle performance of the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C//NVP full cell at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The insets show a button SIBs to light up an LED indicator.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>V<sub>5</sub>S<sub>8−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C and V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C in-plane heterojunctions also demonstrated superior storage performance in sodium-ion batteries. V<sub>5</sub>S<sub>8−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C maintained high discharge capacities of 683.6 and 568.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information) and 500 cycles at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information), respectively. Notably, compared with the pure V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information), this electrode retained high specific capacities of 333.1 and 204.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 and even 100 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information). This ultrafast charge/discharge tolerance and storage performance of VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C and V<sub>5</sub>S<sub>8−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrodes surpassed other VSe<sub>2</sub>- and V<sub>5</sub>S<sub>8</sub>-based counterparts (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), as well as most previously reported ex situ out-plane heterojunction and surface coating electrodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0003">3f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0017_R_d176113491e1172" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Nano">17</a>, <a href="#adma202310336-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0046_R_d176113491e1172" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">46</a>-<a href="#adma202310336-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0053_R_d176113491e1172" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Nano Energy">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C, although this oxide-based heterojunction exhibits a lower storage capacity than selenide- and sulfide-based cases, it still demonstrates exemplary storage capability with specific capacities of 263.1, 240.6, 212.4, 158.5, and 104.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information). The resulting performance surpasses that of many other reported vanadium oxide-based electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a>, Supporting Information). Given its adaptability to VSe<sub>2</sub>, V<sub>5</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, and V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, this methodology is expected to extend to other advanced material system designs by selecting suitable MXene templates and optimizing process parameters. To further demonstrate the practical application feasibility of the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C in-plane heterojunction, sodium-ion full cells are constructed using VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C as the anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) as the cathode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information). The specific capacity of full cells, calculated based on the mass of NVP cathode material, is depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0003">3g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a> (Supporting Information), showcasing the rate performance test and corresponding GCD curves of the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C//NVP full cell. The specific capacities of 93, 85.8, 76.4, 65.1, and 46.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> are delivered at current densities of 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. In addition, the cycle performance of full cells is evaluated at a current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, revealing that a specific capacity of 58.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is retained even after 1000 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0003">3h</a>). Furthermore, a specific capacity of 81.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is retained at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a>, Supporting Information). As a proof of concept, the insets show that such button sodium-ion full cell can provide stable and enough power output to light a large red LED light board, with the help of a booster module. Apart from room temperature, the storage performance of such a full cell is also evaluated under cryogenic conditions. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31</a> (Supporting Information), a high discharge capacity of 67.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be achieved after 450 cycles at the working conditions of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 0&nbsp;°C, demonstrating good low-temperature electrochemical performance.</p>
         
         <p>In situ XRD analysis is conducted to explore the storage mechanism of the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0004">4a</a> presents the discharge process, showing a multistep phase transition characteristic. Initially, the characteristic peaks of VSe<sub>2</sub> gradually weaken and disappear with the embedding of Na ions. Subsequently, distinct peaks appear at angles of 36.17° and 62.77°, corresponding to the (015) and (1013) crystal planes of the Na<sub>0.6</sub>VSe<sub>2</sub> phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310336-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310336-bib-0017_R_d176113491e1283" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Nano">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the discharge progresses, the characteristic peaks of Na<sub>0.6</sub>VSe<sub>2</sub> gradually weaken, accompanied by new peaks at angles such as 32.62° and 57°, representing the (200) and (113) crystal faces of NaVSe<sub>2</sub>. Finally, the disappearance of partial NaVSe<sub>2</sub> peaks and the simultaneous formation of the (220) crystal face of Na<sub>2</sub>Se at 37.24° are observed. In the opposite charging process, several diffraction peaks of the VSe<sub>2</sub> phase, such as at 29.22° and 60.59° would reform when charged to 3.0&nbsp;V. This in-situ analysis revealed the partial reversibility of VSe<sub>2</sub> upon phase transition due to the decreased crystallinity of the transition phases. Simultaneously, the morphological evolution of electrodes upon Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction is analyzed. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0004">4b–d</a> displays the FESEM image, HRTEM image and corresponding EDS elemental mapping of VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C after discharge to 0.01&nbsp;V, respectively. The original nanosheet-like morphology is retained. In addition, lattice fringes are observed corresponding to Na<sub>2</sub>Se (200) and V(110), indicating phase transition from VSe<sub>2</sub> to Na<sub>2</sub>Se upon sodiation. By contrast, lattice fringes corresponding to VSe<sub>2</sub> (003) at a charging voltage of 3.0&nbsp;V indicate the reversible phase transition upon Na<sup>+</sup> extraction (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0004">4e–g</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202310336-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5a9f9e4a-d734-457b-9dc3-2fdd8722982f/adma202310336-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5a9f9e4a-d734-457b-9dc3-2fdd8722982f/adma202310336-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/81b5210f-9f65-4138-98c8-d5f85a2aa0dd/adma202310336-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5a9f9e4a-d734-457b-9dc3-2fdd8722982f/adma202310336-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310336-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310336" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Study on structural and morphological evolution of VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode upon cycling. a) In situ XRD patterns were recorded in the first cycle and corresponding charge-discharge curves. FESEM and HRTEM images and the corresponding EDS mapping of VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode when b–d) discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V and e–g) charged to 3.0&nbsp;V, respectively. h) Ex situ XRD patterns were recorded in the fifth cycle. FESEM images of i) VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C and j) VSe<sub>2</sub> electrode after 100 cycles at the current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The ex situ XRD pattern of the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode at the fifth cycle indicates the evolution of active phases during cycling. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0004">4h</a> indicates that, compared to the first lap, the diffraction peaks of VSe<sub>2</sub> disappear after a few cycles. The Na<sub>x</sub>VSe<sub>2</sub> phase dominates the electrochemical cycling. Moreover, the Na<sub>0.6</sub>VSe<sub>2</sub> and NaVSe<sub>2</sub> phases disappear when discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V, accompanied by the formation of the Na<sub>2</sub>Se phase. In subsequent cycles, only the NaVSe<sub>2</sub> phase is observed after charging to 3.0&nbsp;V, indicating a transition from low crystallinity VSe<sub>2</sub> to a stable Na<sub>x</sub>VSe<sub>2</sub> phase as the dominant phase. Beyond that, similar phase transition tendency also can be detected in the tenth cycle, which further confirms the reversibility of such storage mechanism (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32</a>, Supporting Information). The in situ/ex situ XRD analysis reveals the storage mechanism of partial VSe<sub>2</sub> reversible conversion and highly reversible Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VSe<sub>2</sub> phase conversion during cycling. Finally, the structural integrity of the electrodes is examined. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0004">4i,j</a> shows the FESEM images of VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C and VSe<sub>2</sub> electrodes after 100 cycles, respectively. As revealed, such in-plane heterojunction electrode retains a robust accordion-like structure. In contrast, severe structural collapse is observed in the bulk VSe<sub>2</sub> electrode, primarily attributed to the considerable volumetric strain effect-induced interfacial conduction disconnection between intermediate phases (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S33</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations are conducted to determine the regulating effect of anion vacancy-enriched in-plane heterojunction on the reaction kinetics and thermodynamics of the electrode. Two structural models are constructed: one with pure VSe<sub>2</sub> and the other co-modified with Se vacancy and carbon interface (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S34</a>, Supporting Information). The electronic density of states (DOS) is first evaluated to reveal changes in intrinsic structural properties. The total DOS patterns (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S35</a>, Supporting Information) show that VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C possesses a higher electron state at the Fermi level, indicating better electronic conductivity. Regarding Na adsorption behavior, the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C model is investigated in two cases: one near the vacancy (VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C-Na1), and the other above the vacancy (VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C-Na2). The calculated adsorption energy depicted in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0005">5a</a>, along with the optimized structure and corresponding isosurface of the electron density distribution (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S36</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C exhibits a more significant Na atom capture ability than pure VSe<sub>2</sub>, either near or above the vacancy. Differential charge density distribution was analyzed to understand the reason behind it. Note that the yellow and purple regions represent the charge accumulation and consumption. Compared with pure VSe<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0005">5b</a>), an enormous amount of charge accumulation around the adsorbed Na atom and adjacent atoms near the Se vacancy (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S37</a>, Supporting Information) and significantly above the Se vacancy (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0005">5c</a>), confirming a more vital electrostatic interaction between Na and VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C. Crystal orbital Hamilton population (COHP) analysis is further used to evaluate the bonding stability of the Na-intercalated phase. The corresponding results and calculated −ICOHP values are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0005">5d–f</a>, respectively. Although VSe<sub>2</sub> (−ICOHP = 5.68) offers higher bond stability than VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C, it exhibits substantially reduced thermodynamic stability after Na intercalation (−ICOHP = 4.28). By contrast, the structural stability of the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C system can be enhanced by introducing Na atoms, leading to higher −ICOHP values of 4.8 near the vacancy and 4.49 above the vacancy, respectively. According to Na atom adsorption behavior and bonding thermodynamics analyses, the presence of anion vacancy can aid VSe<sub>2</sub> in trapping Na atoms and forming a thermodynamically stable Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VSe<sub>2</sub> phase. Combined with the storage mechanism, this enhances the structural stability of the evolutional active phases and the cycle performance.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202310336-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/09ce2d81-5596-4351-ac38-dc653f8949f3/adma202310336-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/09ce2d81-5596-4351-ac38-dc653f8949f3/adma202310336-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fa93777a-9179-472c-90b8-d3c3a20d41c8/adma202310336-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/09ce2d81-5596-4351-ac38-dc653f8949f3/adma202310336-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310336-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310336" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Theoretical investigations on reaction thermodynamics and kinetics of electrodes. a) Adsorption energy of VSe<sub>2</sub>-Na, VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C-Na1 and VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C-Na2. Crystal structure and differential charge density distributionof b) VSe<sub>2</sub>-Na and c) VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C-Na2 from the side view, respectively. COHP analysis of the d) VSe<sub>2</sub> and e) VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C before and after Na adsorption, as well as their f) corresponding ICOHP values. The top view of the Na diffusion path in the interlayer of g) VSe<sub>2</sub> and h,i) VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C heterojunction, as well as the j–l) corresponding energy barrier.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Furthermore, the reaction kinetics is analyzed. The side view (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S38</a>, Supporting Information) and top view (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0005">5g–i</a>) of Na atom diffusion along the interlayer of VSe<sub>2</sub> and VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C are presented, along with their corresponding diffusion energy barrier (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0005">5j–l</a>). In particular, the Na atom undergoes isotropic random diffusion through the interlayer of VSe<sub>2</sub> with a high energy barrier of 0.227&nbsp;eV. However, anisotropic preferential migration occurs near the Se vacancy (0.189&nbsp;eV) and above the Se vacancy (0.106&nbsp;eV) through VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C with a substantially reduced energy barrier. The reaction kinetics are enhanced by the synergistic effect of anion vacancy and carbon interface engineering, which delocalize the interfacial charge distribution and provide additional channels for Na atom diffusion. Such a VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C in-plane heterojunction with built-in bifunctional anion vacancy exhibits higher reaction thermodynamic stability and faster reaction kinetics than pure VSe<sub>2</sub>, leading to superfast and reversible storage capability.</p>
         
         <p>In addition, molecular dynamics (MD) simulation is employed to compare the diffusion of Na atoms in the two electrode systems. The initial simulation boxes comprise the electrolyte and electrode materials (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S39</a>, Supporting Information). The electrolyte consists of 6 NaPF<sub>6</sub> molecules dissolved in 86 DME molecules to simulate the electrolyte environment practically. Snapshots of MD simulation after Na atoms diffusion in the VSe<sub>2</sub> and VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C systems are displayed in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0006">6a,b</a>, respectively. The VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C case shows a faster transport behavior since more Na atoms can move near the electrode surface during the same exercise time. Moreover, the corresponding overall Na atoms diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) is calculated. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0006">6c</a>, the value of <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> (3.95 × 10<sup>−4</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) in the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode system is more than twice that in the VSe<sub>2</sub> system (1.69 × 10<sup>−4</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>). According to the results of DFT analysis, this accelerated transport behavior is a result of optimizing reaction kinetics and thermodynamics at the electrode–electrolyte interface.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202310336-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c7ea7161-b9fc-4691-b2e4-abf559efdc65/adma202310336-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c7ea7161-b9fc-4691-b2e4-abf559efdc65/adma202310336-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f5117cbc-9a3f-4f38-ac25-241cafdde2cc/adma202310336-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c7ea7161-b9fc-4691-b2e4-abf559efdc65/adma202310336-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310336-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310336" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Investigation on the storage kinetics of the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode. MD simulation snapshots for Na atoms diffusion in the a) VSe<sub>2</sub> and b) VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode in the electrolyte of 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>/DME. c) Overall Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient. d) CV curves were recorded at various scanning rates. e) Pseudocapacitive contribution ratio at different scanning rates. f) During the discharge–charge process, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of VSe<sub>2</sub> and VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrodes. g) Schematic diagram of the superfast and reversible storage performance of VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode, enabled by the multiscale interfacial synergistic effect between built-in bifunctional anion vacancy and in-plane heterostructure.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">Besides that, the reaction kinetics of the electrode are investigated to determine the origin of superior electrochemical performance. First, CV tests are performed at different rates ranging from 0.1 to 1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0006">6d</a>. Notably, the similar redox peaks at the various scan rates are similar, indicating the high reaction reversibility of the VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode. Moreover, the proportion of pseudocapacitive contribution during the storage process can be quantitatively evaluated using the following formula

                              </mrow>
                           </mfrac>
                           <msup>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                 <mfrac>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">V</mi>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">n</mi>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                    </mrow>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi>
                                 </mfrac>
                              </mfenced>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msup>
                           <msup>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                 <mfrac>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">E</mi>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                    </mrow>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">E</mi>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">t</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                    </mrow>
                                 </mfrac>
                              </mfenced>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\rm{D}} = {4 \over {{\rm{\tau \pi }}}}{\left( {{{{{\rm{V}}}_{\rm{M}}{{\rm{n}}}_{\rm{M}}} \over {\rm{S}}}} \right)}^2{\left( {{{\Delta {{\rm{E}}}_{\rm{s}}} \over {\Delta {{\rm{E}}}_{\rm{t}}}}} \right)}^2 \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0006">6f</a> depicts the calculated <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of electrodes upon discharge and charge. The VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode facilitates substantially faster ion diffusion than VSe<sub>2</sub>. The <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of the heterojunction electrode ranges from 8.7 × 10<sup>−11</sup> to 1.2 × 10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which is nearly two orders of magnitude larger than that in the pure VSe<sub>2</sub> system (8.02 × 10<sup>−13</sup> and 1.05 × 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>), again demonstrating the boosted ion transport kinetics. Therefore, the superfast and reversible sodium storage capability of the in-plane heterojunction electrode with built-in bifunctional anion vacancy is illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310336-fig-0006">6g</a>. Constructing an in-plane heterojunction can maximize the face contact of binary components at the mesoscale level. At the microscale level, the conductive carbon interphase significantly improves the interfacial electron transfer and ensures high conductivity of the Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VSe<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>Se/V phases. Moreover, abundant anion vacancies at the atomic level play a bifunctional role in regulating the reaction kinetics and thermodynamics, providing additional ion transport channels, lowering the ion diffusion energy barrier, and increasing the structural stability of the Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VSe<sub>2</sub> phase. Such multiscale synergies contribute to the well-maintained heterostructure of electrodes with exceptional storage performance.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202310336-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202310336-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>This study established a novel design concept featuring an in-plane heterojunction with an inherent anion vacancy, realized through in situ selenization and self-limited growth on the versatile V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene template. This innovation unlocks the potential of robust VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries, extending its applicability to V<sub>5</sub>S<sub>8−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C and V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C in-plane heterojunctions, suggesting a universal approach. The VSe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>/C electrode exhibits exceptional sodium-ion storage capabilities, retaining a high discharge capacity of 424.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at a current density of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>. In addition, it exhibits remarkable fast-charging efficiency under extreme conditions, with reversible capacities of 392.5, 363.9, and 353.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> achieved at 50, 80, and even 100 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively—a performance unparalleled in current literature. Our study combines comprehensive theoretical and experimental investigations to unveil the structure–activity mechanism underpinning these exceptional results. The synergistic effects of a bifunctional Se anion vacancy and conductive carbon interface engineering at multiple scales enable the integrated regulation of volumetric strain effects, sluggish reaction kinetics, and unstable conversion thermodynamics within the electrode. This work offers a novel perspective for increasing the storage capacity of TMD electrodes through the integrated modulation of multiscale structural properties, thereby advancing the development of ultrafast-charging sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
